<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1134653256">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1092331828">@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .contains-special-characters{width:22em}}</style><div class="side-box metadata side-box-right contains-special-characters noprint selfref"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style>
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/Shalom" title="Shalom"><img alt="שָׁלוֹם" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/40px-Shalom2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="22" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/60px-Shalom2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Shalom2.svg/80px-Shalom2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="741" data-file-height="400" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew text</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Hebrew" title="Help:Hebrew">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of Hebrew letters.</div></div>
</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p><p><b>Modern Hebrew</b> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020748213"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">עִבְרִית חֲדָשָׁה</span>&#8206;</span></span> <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">ʿĪvrīt ḥadašá</i></span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="he-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Hebrew" title="Help:IPA/Hebrew">&#91;ivˈʁit<span class="wrap"> </span>χadaˈʃa&#93;</a></span>), also called <b>Israeli Hebrew</b> or simply <b>Hebrew</b>, is the standard form of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew language</a> spoken today. Developed as part of <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">Hebrew's revival</a> in the late 19th century and early 20th century, it is the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">State of Israel</a>, and the world's only <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite language</a> in use. Coinciding with the creation of the state of Israel, where it is the <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a>, Modern Hebrew is the only successful instance of a complete <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">language revival</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GrenobleWhaley2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrenobleWhaley2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GrenobleWhaley_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GrenobleWhaley-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hebrew, a <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic language</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic language family</a>, was spoken since antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish people</a> until the 3rd century BCE, when it was supplanted by <a href="/wiki/Western_Aramaic_languages" title="Western Aramaic languages">Western Aramaic</a>, a dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic language</a>. Although Hebrew continued to be used for <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish liturgy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_literature" title="Jewish literature">poetry and literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Letter_(message)" title="Letter (message)">written correspondence</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> it became <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">extinct</a> as a spoken language. 
</p><p>By the late 19th century, Russian-Jewish linguist <a href="/wiki/Eliezer_Ben-Yehuda" title="Eliezer Ben-Yehuda">Eliezer Ben-Yehuda</a> had begun a popular movement to <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">revive</a> Hebrew as a living language, motivated by his desire to preserve <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_literature" title="Hebrew literature">Hebrew literature</a> and a distinct <a href="/wiki/Jewish_peoplehood" title="Jewish peoplehood">Jewish nationality</a> in the context of <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Currently, Hebrew is spoken by approximately 9–10 million people, counting native, fluent, and non-fluent speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-israel-hayom-hebrew-speakers_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-israel-hayom-hebrew-speakers-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Behadrey-Haredim_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Behadrey-Haredim-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Half of this figure comprises Israelis who speak it as their native language, while the other half is split: 1.5 million are immigrants to Israel; 1.5 million are <a href="/wiki/Arab_citizens_of_Israel" title="Arab citizens of Israel">Israeli Arabs</a>, whose first language is usually <a href="/wiki/Levantine_Arabic" title="Levantine Arabic">Arabic</a>; and half a million are <a href="/wiki/Yerida" title="Yerida">expatriate Israelis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">diaspora Jews</a>.
</p><p>Under Israeli law, the organization that officially directs the development of Modern Hebrew is the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_the_Hebrew_Language" title="Academy of the Hebrew Language">Academy of the Hebrew Language</a>, headquartered at the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">Hebrew University of Jerusalem</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most common scholarly term for the language is "Modern Hebrew" (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">עברית חדשה</span></span> <i>ʿivrít ħadašá[h]</i>). Most people refer to it simply as <i>Hebrew</i> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">עברית</span></span> <i>Ivrit</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Dekel1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dekel1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Modern Hebrew" has been described as "somewhat problematic"<sup id="cite_ref-Matras1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matras1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> as it implies unambiguous <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> from <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Matras1_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matras1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Haiim_B._Ros%C3%A9n&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Haiim B. Rosén (page does not exist)">Haiim B. Rosén</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://he.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D7%97%D7%99%D7%99%D7%9D_%D7%A8%D7%95%D7%96%D7%9F" class="extiw" title="he:חיים רוזן">he</a>&#93;</span> (חיים רוזן) supported the now widely used<sup id="cite_ref-Matras1_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matras1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> term "Israeli Hebrew" on the basis that it "represented the non-chronological nature of Hebrew".<sup id="cite_ref-Dekel1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dekel1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rosen15_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rosen15-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, Israeli linguist <a href="/wiki/Ghil%27ad_Zuckermann" title="Ghil&#39;ad Zuckermann">Ghil'ad Zuckermann</a> proposed the term "Israeli" to represent the multiple origins of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 325">&#58;&#8202;325&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dekel1_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dekel1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew language</a></div>
<p>The history of the Hebrew language can be divided into four major periods:<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>, until about the 3rd century BCE; the language of most of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic Hebrew</a>, the language of the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Medieval_Hebrew" title="Medieval Hebrew">Medieval Hebrew</a>, from about the 6th to the 13th century CE</li>
<li>Modern Hebrew, the language of the modern State of Israel</li></ul>
<p>Jewish contemporary sources describe Hebrew flourishing as a spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">kingdoms of Israel and Judah</a>, during about 1200 to 586 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Scholars debate the degree to which Hebrew remained a spoken vernacular following the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Babylonian captivity</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Old_Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Aramaic language">Old Aramaic</a> became the predominant international language in the region.
</p><p>Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">died out</a> as a vernacular language somewhere between 200 and 400 CE, declining after the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a> of 132–136 CE, which devastated the population of <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>. After <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Jews in the Roman Empire">the exile</a>, Hebrew became restricted to <a href="/wiki/Liturgical" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical">liturgical</a> and literary use.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival">Revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Revival"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">Revival of the Hebrew language</a></div><p>Hebrew had been spoken at various times and for a number of purposes throughout the Diaspora, and during the <a href="/wiki/Old_Yishuv" title="Old Yishuv">Old Yishuv</a> it had developed into a spoken <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> among the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Jews" title="Palestinian Jews">Jews of Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eliezer_Ben-Yehuda" title="Eliezer Ben-Yehuda">Eliezer Ben-Yehuda</a> then led a <a href="/wiki/Revival_of_the_Hebrew_language" title="Revival of the Hebrew language">revival of the Hebrew language</a> as a mother tongue in the late 19th century and early 20th century.
</p><p>Modern Hebrew used Biblical Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>, Mishnaic spelling and grammar, and Sephardic pronunciation. Many <a href="/wiki/Idiomatic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Idiomatic language">idioms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calques</a> were made from <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Its acceptance by the early Jewish immigrants to <a href="/wiki/Mutasarrifate_of_Jerusalem" title="Mutasarrifate of Jerusalem">Ottoman Palestine</a> was caused primarily by support from the organisations of <a href="/wiki/Edmond_James_de_Rothschild" title="Edmond James de Rothschild">Edmond James de Rothschild</a> in the 1880s and the official status it received in the 1922 constitution of the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Palestine" title="Mandate for Palestine">British Mandate for Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harshav1999_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harshav1999-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Ben-Yehuda codified and planned Modern Hebrew using 8,000 words from the Bible and 20,000 words from rabbinical commentaries. Many new words were borrowed from Arabic, due to the language's common Semitic roots with Hebrew, but changed to fit Hebrew phonology and grammar, for example the words <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">gerev</i></span> (sing.) and <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">garbayim</i></span> (pl.) are now applied to 'socks', a diminutive of the Arabic <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ğuwārib</i></span> ('socks').<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In addition, early Jewish immigrants, borrowing from the local Arabs, and later immigrants from Arab lands introduced many nouns as loanwords from Arabic (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Mentha" title="Mentha">nana</a></i>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Zaatar" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaatar">zaatar</a></i></span>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Apricot" title="Apricot">mishmish</a></i></span>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Coriander" title="Coriander">kusbara</a></i></span>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hilbah" class="mw-redirect" title="Hilbah">ḥilba</a></i></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vigna_unguiculata_subsp._unguiculata" class="mw-redirect" title="Vigna unguiculata subsp. unguiculata">lubiya</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hummus" title="Hummus">hummus</a></i>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Carrot" title="Carrot">gezer</a></i></span>, <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Basil" title="Basil">rayḥan</a></i></span>, etc.), as well as much of Modern Hebrew's slang. Despite Ben-Yehuda's fame as the renewer of Hebrew, the most productive renewer of Hebrew words was poet <a href="/wiki/Haim_Nahman_Bialik" class="mw-redirect" title="Haim Nahman Bialik">Haim Nahman Bialik</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>One of the phenomena seen with the revival of the Hebrew language is that old meanings of nouns were occasionally changed for altogether different meanings, such as <i>bardelas</i> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">ברדלס</span></span>), which in Mishnaic Hebrew meant '<a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">hyena</a>',<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> but in Modern Hebrew it now means '<a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a>'; or <i>shezīph</i> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">שְׁזִיף</span></span>) which is now used for '<a href="/wiki/Plum" title="Plum">plum</a>', but formerly meant '<a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">jujube</a>'.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The word <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">kishū’īm</i></span> (formerly 'cucumbers')<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> is now applied to a variety of <a href="/wiki/Summer_squash" title="Summer squash">summer squash</a> (<i>Cucurbita pepo</i> var. <i>cylindrica</i>), a plant native to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. Another example is the word <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">kǝvīš</i></span> (<span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">כביש</span></span>), which now denotes a street or a road, but is actually an <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> adjective meaning 'trodden down' or 'blazed', rather than a common noun. It was originally used to describe a blazed trail.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The flower <i><a href="/wiki/Anemone_coronaria" title="Anemone coronaria">Anemone coronaria</a></i>, called in Modern Hebrew <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">kalanit</i></span>, was formerly called in Hebrew <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">shoshanat ha-melekh</i></span> ('the king's flower').<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For a simple comparison between the Sephardic and Yemenite versions of Mishnaic Hebrew, see <a href="/wiki/Yemenite_Hebrew#Distinctive_pronunciations_preserved" title="Yemenite Hebrew">Yemenite Hebrew</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern Hebrew is classified as an <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic family</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a> subgroup.<sup id="cite_ref-e18_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weninger,_Stefan_2011_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weninger,_Stefan_2011-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> While Modern Hebrew is largely based on <a href="/wiki/Mishnaic_Hebrew" title="Mishnaic Hebrew">Mishnaic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical</a> Hebrew as well as <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Hebrew" title="Sephardi Hebrew">Sephardi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Hebrew" title="Ashkenazi Hebrew">Ashkenazi</a> liturgical and literary tradition from the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Hebrew" title="Medieval Hebrew">Medieval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">Haskalah</a> eras and retains its Semitic character in its morphology and in much of its syntax,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2016)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> the consensus among scholars is that Modern Hebrew represents a fundamentally new linguistic system, not directly continuing any previous linguistic state.<sup id="cite_ref-Reshef,_Yael_2013_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reshef,_Yael_2013-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Hebrew is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koiné language</a> based on historical layers of Hebrew that incorporates foreign elements, mainly those introduced during the most critical revival period between 1880 and 1920, as well as new elements created by speakers through natural linguistic evolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Reshef,_Yael_2013_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reshef,_Yael_2013-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-e18_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> A minority of scholars argue that the revived language had been so influenced by various substrate languages that it is genealogically a hybrid with Indo-European.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Those theories have not been met with general acceptance, and the consensus among a majority of scholars is that Modern Hebrew, despite its non-Semitic influences, can correctly be classified as a Semitic language.<sup id="cite_ref-Weninger,_Stefan_2011_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weninger,_Stefan_2011-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Although European languages have had an impact on Modern Hebrew, the impact may often be overstated.  Although Modern Hebrew has more of the features attributed to <a href="/wiki/Standard_Average_European" title="Standard Average European">Standard Average European</a> than Biblical Hebrew, it is still quite distant, and has fewer such features than Modern Standard Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alphabet">Alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Alphabet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cursive_Hebrew" title="Cursive Hebrew">Cursive Hebrew</a></div>
<p>Modern Hebrew is written from right to left using the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew alphabet</a>, which is an <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjad</a>, or consonant-only script of 22 letters based on the "square" letter form, known as <i>Ashurit</i> (Assyrian), which was developed from the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic script</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Cursive_Hebrew" title="Cursive Hebrew">cursive</a> script is used in handwriting. When necessary, vowels are indicated by diacritic marks above or below the letters known as <i><a href="/wiki/Niqqud" title="Niqqud">Nikkud</a></i>, or by use of <i><a href="/wiki/Matres_lectionis#Usage_in_Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Matres lectionis">Matres lectionis</a></i>, which are consonantal letters used as vowels. Further diacritics like <a href="/wiki/Dagesh" title="Dagesh">Dagesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shin_(letter)#Sin_and_Shin_Dot" title="Shin (letter)">Sin and Shin dots</a> are used to indicate variations in the pronunciation of the consonants (e.g. <i>bet</i>/<i>vet</i>, <i>shin</i>/<i>sin</i>). The letters "<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020748213"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">צ׳</span>&#8206;", "<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020748213"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">ג׳</span>&#8206;", "<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1020748213"><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">ז׳</span>&#8206;", each modified with a <a href="/wiki/Geresh" title="Geresh">Geresh</a>, represent the consonants <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless postalveolar affricate">t͡ʃ</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>&#93;</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>&#93;</span>. The consonant <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless postalveolar affricate">t͡ʃ</a>&#93;</span> may also be written as "תש" and "טש". <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labial%E2%80%93velar_approximant" title="Voiced labial–velar approximant">w</a>&#93;</span> is represented interchangeably by a simple vav "ו", non-standard double vav "וו" and sometimes by non-standard geresh modified vav "ו׳".
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew_phonology" title="Modern Hebrew phonology">Modern Hebrew phonology</a></div>
<p>Modern Hebrew has fewer phonemes than Biblical Hebrew but it has developed its own phonological complexity. Israeli Hebrew has 25 to 27 consonants, depending on whether the speaker has pharyngeals. It has 5 to 10 vowels, depending on whether diphthongs and vowels are counted, varying with the speaker and the analysis.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Morphology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> (formation, structure, and interrelationship of words in a language) is essentially <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Modern Hebrew showcases much of the inflectional morphology of the classical upon which it was based. In the formation of new words, all verbs and the majority of nouns and adjectives are formed by the classically Semitic devices of <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">triconsonantal roots</a> (<i>shoresh</i>) with <a href="/wiki/Nonconcatenative_morphology" title="Nonconcatenative morphology">affixed patterns</a> (<i>mishkal</i>). Mishnaic attributive patterns are often used to create nouns, and Classical patterns are often used to create adjectives. Blended words are created by merging two bound stems or parts of words.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Syntax"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Hebrew_grammar" title="Modern Hebrew grammar">Modern Hebrew grammar</a></div>
<p>The syntax of Modern Hebrew is mainly Mishnaic<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but also shows the influence of different contact languages to which its speakers have been exposed during the revival period and over the past century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">Word order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Word order"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word order of Modern Hebrew is predominately SVO (<a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–verb–object">subject–verb–object</a>). Biblical Hebrew was originally <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object" class="mw-redirect" title="Verb–subject–object">verb–subject–object</a> (VSO), but drifted into SVO.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Modern Hebrew maintains classical syntactic properties associated with VSO languages: it is <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">prepositional</a>, rather than postpositional, in making case and adverbial relations, <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_verb" title="Auxiliary verb">auxiliary verbs</a> precede main verbs; main verbs precede their complements, and noun modifiers (<a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Determiner" title="Determiner">determiners</a> other than the <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a> <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">ה-</span></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Noun_adjunct" title="Noun adjunct">noun adjuncts</a>) follow the head noun; and in <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> constructions, the possessee noun precedes the possessor. Moreover, Modern Hebrew allows and sometimes requires sentences with a predicate initial.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexicon">Lexicon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lexicon"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern Hebrew has expanded its vocabulary effectively to meet the needs of casual vernacular, of science and technology, of journalism and <a href="/wiki/Belles-lettres" title="Belles-lettres">belles-lettres</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Ghil%27ad_Zuckermann" title="Ghil&#39;ad Zuckermann">Ghil'ad Zuckermann</a>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The number of attested Biblical Hebrew words is 8198, of which some 2000 are <a href="/wiki/Hapax_legomenon" title="Hapax legomenon">hapax legomena</a> (the number of Biblical Hebrew roots, on which many of these words are based, is 2099). The number of attested Rabbinic Hebrew words is less than 20,000, of which (i) 7879 are Rabbinic par excellence, i.e. they did not appear in the Old Testament (the number of new Rabbinic Hebrew roots is 805); (ii) around 6000 are a subset of Biblical Hebrew; and (iii) several thousand are Aramaic words which can have a Hebrew form. Medieval Hebrew added 6421 words to (Modern) Hebrew. The approximate number of new lexical items in Israeli is 17,000 (cf. 14,762 in Even-Shoshan 1970 [...]). With the inclusion of foreign and technical terms [...], the total number of Israeli words, including words of biblical, rabbinic and medieval descent, is more than 60,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuckermann_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckermann-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 64–65">&#58;&#8202;64–65&#8202;</span></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loanwords">Loanwords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Modern_Hebrew&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Loanwords"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern Hebrew has loanwords from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (both from the local <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Arabic" title="Palestinian Arabic">Palestinian dialect</a> and from the <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Judeo-Arabic languages">dialects of Jewish immigrants from Arab countries</a>), <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">Yiddish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish" title="Judaeo-Spanish">Judaeo-Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and other languages. Simultaneously, Israeli Hebrew makes use of words that were originally loanwords from the languages of surrounding nations from ancient times: Canaanite languages as well as Akkadian. Mishnaic Hebrew borrowed many nouns from Aramaic (including Persian words borrowed by Aramaic), as well as from Greek and to a lesser extent Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In the Middle Ages, Hebrew made heavy semantic borrowing from Arabic, especially in the fields of science and philosophy. Here are typical examples of Hebrew loanwords:
</p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFMandel2005" class="citation book cs1">Mandel, George (2005). "Ben-Yehuda, Eliezer [Eliezer Yizhak Perelman] (1858–1922)". <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/57470923"><i>Encyclopedia of modern Jewish culture</i></a>. Glenda Abramson ([New ed.]&#160;ed.). London. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-415-29813-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-415-29813-X"><bdi>0-415-29813-X</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/57470923">57470923</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20230701134739/https://www.worldcat.org/title/57470923">Archived</a> from the original on 1 July 2023<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 May</span> 2023</span>. <q>In 1879 he wrote an article for the Hebrew press advocating Jewish immigration to Palestine. Ben-Yehuda argued that only in a country with a Jewish majority could a living Hebrew literature and a distinct Jewish nationality survive; elsewhere, the pressure to assimilate to the language of the majority would cause Hebrew to die out. Shortly afterwards he reached the conclusion that the active use of Hebrew as a literary language could not be sustained, notwithstanding the hoped-for concentration of Jews in Palestine, unless Hebrew also became the everyday spoken language there.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Ben-Yehuda%2C+Eliezer+%5BEliezer+Yizhak+Perelman%5D+%281858%E2%80%931922%29&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+modern+Jewish+culture&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.edition=%5BNew+ed.%5D&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F57470923&amp;rft.isbn=0-415-29813-X&amp;rft.aulast=Mandel&amp;rft.aufirst=George&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.worldcat.org%2Foclc%2F57470923&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AModern+Hebrew" class="Z3988"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_book" title="Template:Cite book">cite book</a>}}</code>:  CS1 maint: location missing publisher (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_location_missing_publisher" title="Category:CS1 maint: location missing publisher">link</a>)</span></span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 November</span> 2013</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Israel+Hayom&amp;rft.atitle=A+million+and+a+half+Israelis+struggle+with+Hebrew&amp;rft.date=2013-03-18&amp;rft.aulast=Klein&amp;rft.aufirst=Zeev&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.israelhayom.com%2Fsite%2Fnewsletter_article.php%3Fid%3D8065&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AModern+Hebrew" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Behadrey-Haredim-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Behadrey-Haredim_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFNachman_GurBehadrey_Haredim" class="citation web cs1">Nachman Gur; Behadrey Haredim. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131104025104/http://www.bhol.co.il/article_en.aspx?id=52405">"Kometz Aleph&#160;– Au• How many Hebrew speakers are there in the world?"</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bhol.co.il/article_en.aspx?id=52405">the original</a> on 4 November 2013<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 November</span> 2013</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Kometz+Aleph+%E2%80%93+Au%E2%80%A2+How+many+Hebrew+speakers+are+there+in+the+world%3F&amp;rft.au=Nachman+Gur&amp;rft.au=Behadrey+Haredim&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bhol.co.il%2Farticle_en.aspx%3Fid%3D52405&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AModern+Hebrew" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Dekel1-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Dekel1_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dekel1_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Dekel1_16-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDekel2014">Dekel 2014</a>; quote: "Most people refer to Israeli Hebrew simply as Hebrew. Hebrew is a broad term, which includes Hebrew as it was spoken and written in different periods of time and according to most of the researchers as it is spoken and written in Israel and elsewhere today. Several names have been proposed for the language spoken in Israel nowadays, Modern Hebrew is the most common one, addressing the latest spoken language variety in Israel (Berman 1978, Saenz-Badillos 1993:269, Coffin-Amir &amp; Bolozky 2005, Schwarzwald 2009:61). The emergence of a new language in Palestine at the end of the nineteenth century was associated with debates regarding the characteristics of that language.... Not all scholars supported the term Modern Hebrew for the new language. Rosén (1977:17) rejected the term Modern Hebrew, since linguistically he claimed that 'modern' should represent a linguistic entity that should command autonomy towards everything that preceded it, while this was not the case in the new emerging language. He also rejected the term Neo-Hebrew, because the prefix 'neo' had been previously used for Mishnaic and Medieval Hebrew (Rosén 1977:15–16), additionally, he rejected the term Spoken Hebrew as one of the possible proposals (Rosén 1977:18). Rosén supported the term Israeli Hebrew as in his opinion it represented the non-chronological nature of Hebrew, as well as its territorial independence (Rosén 1977:18). Rosén then adopted the term Contemporary Hebrew from Téne (1968) for its neutrality, and suggested the broadening of this term to Contemporary Israeli Hebrew (Rosén 1977:19)"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Matras1-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Matras1_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Matras1_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Matras1_17-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMatrasSchiff2005">Matras &amp; Schiff 2005</a>; quote: The language with which we are concerned in this contribution is also known by the names Contemporary Hebrew and Modern Hebrew, both somewhat problematic terms as they rely on the notion of an unambiguous periodization separating Classical or Biblical Hebrew from the present-day language. We follow instead the now widely-used label coined by Rosén (1955), Israeli Hebrew, to denote the link between the emergence of a Hebrew vernacular and the emergence of an Israeli national identity in Israel/Palestine in the early twentieth century."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Rosen15-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Rosen15_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFHaiim_Rosén1977" class="citation book cs1">Haiim Rosén (1 January 1977). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=PydwqOHyCMAC&amp;pg=PA29"><i>Contemporary Hebrew</i></a>. Walter de Gruyter. pp.&#160;15–18. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-3-11-080483-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-3-11-080483-6"><bdi>978-3-11-080483-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Contemporary+Hebrew&amp;rft.pages=15-18&amp;rft.pub=Walter+de+Gruyter&amp;rft.date=1977-01-01&amp;rft.isbn=978-3-11-080483-6&amp;rft.au=Haiim+Ros%C3%A9n&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DPydwqOHyCMAC%26pg%3DPA29&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AModern+Hebrew" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Ghil%27ad_Zuckermann" title="Ghil&#39;ad Zuckermann">Zuckermann, G.</a> (1999), "Review of the Oxford English-Hebrew Dictionary", <i>International Journal of Lexicography</i>, Vol. 12, No. 4, pp. 325-346</span>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/259061/Hebrew-language#ref267079">Hebrew language</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150611005453/http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/259061/Hebrew-language#ref267079">Archived</a> 2015-06-11 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> <i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i></span>

<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">אברהם בן יוסף ,מבוא לתולדות הלשון העברית (Avraham ben-Yosef, Introduction to the History of the Hebrew Language), page 38, אור-עם, Tel Aviv, 1981.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sáenz-Badillos, Ángel and John Elwolde: "There is general agreement that two main periods of RH (Rabbinical Hebrew) can be distinguished. The first, which lasted until the close of the Tannaitic era (around 200 CE), is characterized by RH as a spoken language gradually developing into a literary medium in which the Mishnah, Tosefta, <i>baraitot</i> and Tannaitic <i>midrashim</i> would be composed. The second stage begins with the <i><a href="/wiki/Amoraim" title="Amoraim">Amoraim</a></i> and sees RH being replaced by Aramaic as the spoken vernacular, surviving only as a literary language. Then it continued to be used in later rabbinic writings until the tenth century in, for example, the Hebrew portions of the two Talmuds and in midrashic and haggadic literature."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tudor Parfitt; The Contribution of the old Yishuv to the Revival of Hebrew, Journal of Semitic Studies, Volume XXIX, Issue 2, 1 October 1984, Pages 255–265, <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://doi.org/10.1093/jss/XXIX.2.255">https://doi.org/10.1093/jss/XXIX.2.255</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20230701134735/https://academic.oup.com/jss/article-abstract/XXIX/2/255/1687934?redirectedFrom=fulltext">Archived</a> 2023-07-01 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite id="CITEREFSwirski2002" class="citation book cs1">Swirski, Shlomo (11 September 2002). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=2DOPAgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA30"><i>Politics and Education in Israel: Comparisons with the United States</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cf. <li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Zohar Amar, <i>Flora and Fauna in Maimonides' Teachings</i>, Kfar Darom 2015, p. 157, s.v. <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">שזפין</span></span> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.worldcat.org/oclc/783455868">783455868</a>, explained to mean 'jujube' (<i>Ziziphus jujuba</i>); <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Sirilio" title="Solomon Sirilio">Solomon Sirilio</a>'s Commentary of the Jerusalem Talmud, on <i>Kila'im</i> 1:4, s.v. <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span lang="he" dir="rtl">השיזפין</span></span>, which he explained to mean in Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">azufaifas</i></span> ('jujubes'). LXL), explained in German as meaning <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Brustbeerbaum</i></span> ('jujube').</span>

<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compare <a href="/wiki/Rashi" title="Rashi">Rashi</a>'s commentary on Exodus 9:17, where he says the word <span title="Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="he-Latn">mesillah</i></span> is translated in Aramaic <span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language romanization"><i lang="tmr-Latn">oraḥ kevīsha</i></span> ('a blazed trail'), the word <span title="Jewish Babylonian Aramaic (ca. 200-1200 CE)-language romanization"><i lang="tmr-Latn">kevīsh</i></span> being only an adjective or descriptive word, but not a common noun as it is used today. It is said that <a href="/wiki/Ze%27ev_Yavetz" title="Ze&#39;ev Yavetz">Ze'ev Yavetz</a> (1847&#8211;1924) is the one who coined this modern Hebrew word for 'road'. <li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Latin "familia", from which English "family" is derived, entered Mishnaic Hebrew - and thence, Modern Hebrew - as "pamalya" (פמליה) meaning "entourage". (The original Latin "familia" referred both to a prominent Roman's family and to his household in general, including the entourage of slaves and freedmen which accompanied him in public - hence, both the English and the Hebrew one are derived from the Latin meaning.)</span>
